DEFlNMflONS
Romania is divided into 41 counties or districts called 'judets' which form the geographical base for
both the territorially deconcentrated central govermment administration and the local government
system. The counties are in turn divided into 2,948 municipalities, including 260 urban communities
and 2,688 rural communities.

ROMANIA
EIOYMT AND SOC&IA PROTECrION PROJECT
Loan and Project Summary
Borrower:           Romania
Benefcdaa:          Ministry of Labor and Social Protection (MOLSP)
Amount:             US$55.4 million equivalent
TerIms:             Twenty years, including a five-year grace period at the BRD standard
variable interest rate.
Project
Objectives:         The project will support the Government's efforts to alleviate unomployment
and poverty, improve the efficiency and affordability of employment and
social protection services, and promote labor productivity and economic
competitiveness. The specific objectives of the project are to: (a) strengthen
the capacity of labor offices to administer increasing mumbers of claims for
unemployment benefits and active labor adjustment services (e.g., job
placement, employment counseling, training); (b) develop a flexible adult
training system which responds to evolving labor market demand resulting
from economic restructuring; and (c) implement reforms in social insurance
and assistance programs, targeting those population groups which are most
vulnerable to falling into poverty during and after the transition to a market
economy.
Project
Description:        The project will be implemented over a five-year period by the MOLSP. The
total project cost is estimated at US$81.6 million base cost, and US$95.2
million total cost (including contingencies). The project will finance civil
works, equipment and materials, vehicles, computer hardware and software,
technical assistance, external and local training, and incremental operating
costs. lhe project comprises three interrelated components structured as
fbllows:
I. General Management and Policy: (a) Monitoring and Policy Analysi
of the programs to be introduced under Components II and m below, so that
the MOLSP will have the capability to evaluate and improve the cost-
effectiveness its programs; (b) Automation of the labor and social protection
offices to support and improve the management of programs to be introduced;
and (c) Project Management support to expand and stregthen the already
functioning Project Coordination Unit established under the Technical
Assistance and Critical Imports (TACI) Project, enabling support (e.g.,
overall coordination, procurement) to the technical departments responsible for
implementation.



_jj-
II. Eplonyelnt and Training: (a) Employment Services which will
improve the organization and delivery of services in the labor offices,
including development of Industrial Adjustment Services to deal with large-
scale layoffs; (b) Career Information and Counseling which will develop
modem materials and techniques to support occupational counseling in labor
offices and schools; and (c) Adult Trainig for employed and unemployed
workers to reorient and improve their skdlls in a market-oriented environment.
m. Sodal Protection: (a) Sodal Insurance reforms, based upon a receady
completed government White Paper, which will restructure the social
insurance agency to unify the various pension funds and improve its financial
management, contributions records system and benefit disbursements; and
(b) Social Assistance reforms, also based upon a recently completed
government White Paper, which will develop an integrated system of cash
benefits to replace the currently fragmented system, improve the organization
of social service delivery and target assistance to those most vulnerable to
poverty.
Benenfts
adARbka:            Benefia. The project will significantly assist in the restructuring of the
economy by stengthening the capacity to cope with unemployment, improving
labor mobility, and providing the new skills needed in a market economy.
Upon completion of the project, Romania will have in place an employment
service and job training system which will quickly respond to shifting market
needs. The MOLSP will improve Its organization and management
capabilities through the automation and project monitoring to be put into
place. The social safety net, which the project will help build up, will
strengthen the political will needed to pursue economic reforms during the
period when poverty and other social problems intensify. These benefits from
the social safety net are not just of a humantarian nature but, as demonstrated
by the experience of OECD countries, represent a significant investment in
human resources and contribute to financial savings by avoiding more serious
problems resulting from neglect of pressing social needs.
Risk. A major risk to successful implementation of the project is that the
present implementaion capacity of the MOLSP is insufficiently developed
relative to the complexity of the tasks of building up both reactive and
proactive employment and social protection programs. Although MOLSP has
now acquired implementation experience in the smaller-scale TACI Project,
this much larger project will require strengthening the implementation capacity
of the MOLSP. The implementation plan incorporate lessons learned in the
TACI Project, and appropriate and sufficient amouns of TA and training,
focusing on helping the MOLSP make the transition to largescale project
management. A project supervision plan has been formulated to assist the
MOLSP in following Bank guidelines by helping in the preparation of
standard bidding and contract documents. Nonetheless, intense supervision
from headquarters and the resident mission will be required to minimize the
risk posed by weak implementation capacity.



A second important risk to successful implementation is that of inadequate
financing for the incremental operating costs, especially for additional staffing
required under the Employment Services Component. This has been a
problem in other countries in the region, especially as unemployment rises
sharply and Government budgets become increasingly constrained, where
additional staff budgets are not allocated to increase and improve the quality
of services. Ibis risk is being addressed by explicitly highlighting the need
for incremental staffing, including it in the project cost esdmates, and securing
the commitment of both the Ministry of Finance and the MOLSP to adequate
staffing levels.

ROMANIA
EMPLOYMENT AND SOCIAL PROTECTION PROJECr
I. ECONOMIC REFORM AND THE SOCIAL SAFErY NET
A.    Introductio
1.1   Romania's economy, after a decade of stagnation in the 1980s, has deteriorated dramatically
over the past four years since the end of the Ceausescu era in December 1989. Output has dropped
by 33 percent, and infladon has risen drastically, reaching 200 percent in 1992 and 295 percent in
1993. Although inflation appeared to be declining in early 1994, the level of unemployment rose to
about 11 percent - a level which will continue to increase as the process of economic restructuring is
undertaken. Improvements in economic performance and social welfare will require a munber of
difficult structural adjustments to overcome the economic policy distortions of the Ceausescu era, such
as the forced industrialization of the economy and artificially maintained full employment. One result
of these policy distortions has been that the majority of public enterprises are very poorly prepared to
operate in a market economy. As in the past, they are overstaffed, employ obsolete technology, and
are managed with little regard for market incentives. Moreover, the private sector remains small, and
its limited growth under the slow pace of reforms has not yet substantially contributed to net job
creation. Problems of structural unemployment, which are already developing, will undoubtedly
continue to worsen.
1.2   The current social protection system, which was designed for a socialist, full employment
economy, and met only the special needs of individuals, such as widows, orphans or the handicapped,
has also been overwhelmed by developments over the past four years. This outdated system cannot
cope with the social consequences of structural adjustment. Although living standards may well rise
in the medium to long run as structural adjustment proceeds, the Government recognizes the urgency
of developing new active labor market policies and social safety net programs that aim to help
alleviate unemployment and poverty during and after the transition period.
B.    The Current System of Employment Services and Social Protection
1.3   There are two broad categories of cash benefits provided by the current system - those based
on contributions by beneficiaries, such as unemployment benefits and pensions, and those funded out
of the state budget, such as child allowances and social assistance benefits. These benefits are
administered mainly by the Ministry of Labor and Social Protection (MOLSP) with some involvement
from enterprises for social insurance benefits, and from local governments for social assistance
benefits. Most of these benefits, especially the pro-natalist ones to encourage larger families, were
inherited from the communist era, but others have been put in place since 1990, such as the
unemployment benefit (UB) scheme which became law in December 1990 and was extended in 1992.
Cash benefits and services for the handicapped were created and their administration transferred from
MOLSP to the Secretariat for the Handicapped in 1991; other benefits were reformed (child
allowance, social canteens). There were no means-tested cash benefits programs targeted on poor
households.



-2-
1.4    To carry out its programs, the MOLSP has an extensive network of local offices throughout
the country. In each of Romania's 40 districts or counties (judets) there is a MOLSP directorate,
which is the deconcentrated authority of the MOLSP in the judet and has the same structure as the
Ministry (see Annex 1 for an organization chart and detailed description of benefit programs). Most
districts have a main MOLSP office and several branch offices; the Municipality of Bucharest is
divided into seven district offices. All of the 40 judet main offices include sections (and sometimes
separate facilities) for labor (employment), social assistance and pensions services. Including branch
offices, the resulting network totals about 170 labor offices and about 50 social assistance and pension
offices, representing the MOLSP's safety net service delivery points.
1.5    Unenployment benefits (UB). At present, eligibility for UB extends to anyone over 18 years
old who is unemployed due to liquidation or shrinkage of a firm or is a new entrant to the labor
force 1'. The UB scheme is funded by a 5 percent tax on the wage bill of enterprises and a 1 percent
tax on the wages of employees. The level of benefit ranges from 50-60 percent of the annual average
wage in the last job (depending upon the length of time in the labor force) or from 75-85 percent of
the minimum wage if the last wage was below minimum wage. The duration of benefit is 270 days,
with a possible extension of 270 days at a lower rate of benefit. Benefit checks, once approved by
the local labor offices, are delivered through the postal system. The unemployed report to the local
labor offices to register, and must report back at least once a month to confirm their unemployment
status.
1.6     ocial insurance. Social insurance has widespread coverage in Romania. It includes
essentially universal entitlement to old age, disability, and survivors' pensions, as well as sick and
maternity leaves. The system is complicated by the existence of multiple social insurance funds. The
main state pension system covers roughly three quarters of the labor force. There are at least ten
other smaller social insurance funds, the agricultural fund being the most important one. The other
funds attend to specific occupational categories (artists, clergymen, etc.). The average pension benefit
from the main State fund equals about 40 percent of the average gross wage, and the replacement rate
is 52 percent on average (higher for recent retirees). Social insurance benefits are not taxable. They
are funded by a payroll tax, paid mostly by the employer on behalf of the employee. Sick leave and
maternity leave are administered mainly through the enterprises. The Central Office of Pension
Payments (COPP), under the supervision of MOLSP, along with the enterprises and the banking
system, is much more involved in the administration of the old age and disability pensions. The banks
receive the insurance contributions from enterprises which are then transferred to the pension fund
account of MOLSP. Pensioners must visit the Social Protection unit within the local MOLSP office
to register and have their eligibility for pensions approved, but payments are made regularly through
the postal system. In 1992, responsibility for funding and monitoring the first ten days' sick leave
was transferred from the social insurance agency to the enterprises, resulting in a substantial reduction
of expenditures on these benefits.
1.7    Sialaistanc. Social assistance, by contrast, has reduced coverage and consists mostly of
the delivery of cash benefits, most of which are not means tested. In the past, the system included
the provision of expanded social support services, but since the 1970s this function has been reduced.
At present, very limited social support services are offered: they include free meals at social canteens,
[/    Thu Ia fsswi a ae2Uy a mm tod benet an apli oly to unemployed school leve fm low inwom
househods. It iu to be iegratd inw the ne sa easistm  befit schem.



-3-
voluntary placement of children in foster families, placement of the elderly in pensioners' homes and
of juveniles in detention centers, and one center for street children. Each MOLSP district office has a
social assistance unit with an average staff of five; the central unit at the Ministry consists of 16 staff.
The benefits paid through social assistance offices include the allowances for mothers of three or more
children, birth indemnities, benefits for the wives of soldiers, voluntary foster family placement
benefits, and circumstantial benefits for pensioners. Although not based on contributions, the child
allowance for children of State workers was traditionally administered through the enterprises.
Recently, a new law stipulated that the allowance is for all children, not just those whose parents are
State workers, and its administration has been shifted from the enterprises to the social assistance
system. These child allowances account for 80 percent of all social assistance expenditures.
Approval of free meals at social canteens and of quarterly benefits for the destitute is given by social
assistance offices and funded by MOLSP and local government, and these services are administered
by the local authorities. Although the MOLSP is not directly responsible, institutionalization of
children is widely used for children's welfare. In 1991, more than 92,000 children were living in
these institutions which are, like other institutions for the handicapped and the elderly, administered
by other ministries and bodies. No programs exist to assist the working poor.
C.    Labor Market Issues
1.8    A successful transition to a market economy requires the development of functioning labor
markets as well as product and financial markets. During this period of stabilization and
restructuring, labor market problems will intensify. Unemployment will continue to increase.
lnstitutions need to be strengthened to accelerate market adjustment. However, present employment
services are inadequate. In some cases, unemployment will result from large-scale layoffs that affect
whole communities and require special approaches. Active labor market measures, such as retraining,
are also needed for some workers. These issues are discussed in the following paragraphs.
1.9 Rising unemployment and ineffective employment services. By September 1993, labor offices
had a client:staff ratio of 668:1, compared to an average of 100:1 in OECD countries. Consequently,
staff can barely keep up with the most basic activity of processing unemployment claims (see Annex 2
for statistical information on unemployment and the labor force). However, it is not enough merely
to process unemployment claims and let the unemployed fend for themselves without any other kind
of support, especially in countries where unemployment is a new phenomenon. 'here is the need for
more proactive measures, in addition to unemployment benefits, such as effective job development
and placement programs for clients, improved counseling of clients in job search methods, directing
clients who can benefit from retraining to appropriate programs, and screening clients likely to be
successful in self-employment and direcing them to the appropriate small business assistance
agencies. All of these new services require increases in staffing, especially at a time of rising
unemployment, as well as a different skills mix among the staff.
1.10   Private employment services could play a role in helping meet this need. These services are
allowed to operate under Romanian commercial law, which places no more restrictions on them than
on any other commercial venture. It is expected that these services will become providers of
specialized manpower services, as has been the case in other OECD and formerly centrally planned
economies. Private employment services are usually effective in special niches of the labor market,
such as certain professional occupations, and develop on their own based upon private profit
incentives without the need for government subsidies, as long as the legal framework does not hamper
them. However, private employment services cannot profitably provide, in Romania anymore than



-4-
elsewhere in the world, the integrated system of unemployment and social assistance claims
processing and the needed assistance to workers lacking in basic educational background or in need of
extensive, retraining. The existence of capital market and information imperfections and social
externalities provides a theoretical rationale for carefully designed improvements in public
employment services. There is also the practical rationale that the public employment services
already exist, and constitute a significant charge on the public budget while operating ineffectively and
inefficiently, coupled with the reality that it will take some time for an extensive private sector to
fully develop. Through careful improvements in public employment services while allowing private
employment services to continue to develop, Romania should move towards a good balance of private
and public employment services, which will complement rather than compete with one another; this
shift will move Romania into the mainstream practice with OECD and middle-income industrialized
countries.
1.11 Coping with large-scale lavoffs. Although quantitative estimates of future large-scale layoffs
are not easy to obtain, many informed observers conclude that at least 20-25 percent of workers
currently in State enterprises can potentially be considered surplus. This conclusion is plausible
considering that industrial output has dropped by 50 percent since 1989, and industrial employment
has only dropped by 25 percent.2' To date there have not been any large-scale layoffs, but the
MOLSP esimates that, if these enterprises are restructured or privatized, an additional 1.5 million
workers would be threatened with job loss in the process. There is no specific advance notice
legislation pertaining to layoffs of large groups of workers, other than the standard two week notice
applicable to all workers under labor regulations governing hiring and firing. Experience in other
countries clearly shows that adequate advance notice legislation in the case of large-scale layoffs
provides a useful first step in facilitating the smooth adjustment of affected workers. Effective
advance notice legislation is intended to ensure that the employees to be laid off, the enterprise, the
community, and the local authorities are sufficiently forewarned of the actual layoff to allow for early
intervention. Most countries with functioning labor adjustment mechanisms have two to three months
advance notice provisions. Mass layoff techniques, such as bringing together the management and
workers of firms before the layoffs take place, would prove useful in those regions most heavily
affected by unemployment. Professionally trained facilitators can assist management and workers by
preparing profiles of the firm's labor force and how best to assist in their redeployment to other jobs.
However, in addition to appropriate legal measures, effective mass layoff techniques require a set of
functioning proactive employment measures, such as job training or small business assistance
programs, as well as a service delivery mechanism to access these measures effectively, none of
which exist in Romania.
1.12 Structural unemployment and inadequate adult training. The gap between the skills
distribution demanded by the emerging market economy and that supplied by a labor force trained
under central planning has already resulted in structural unemployment (as contrasted with cyclical,

-5-
frictional or seasonal unemployment). lhis is based on the view of the labor market, not just as an
aggregate demand and supply system, but as a system of distinct, intng labor markets that
determine the structure of wages, employment, vacancies and unemployment. Recent research on
labor markets and economic restructuring in Romania shows that unemployment already has a
significant structural component, in addition to unemployment from the drop in aggregate demand.>
While sound macroeconomic policy is needed to address aggregate demand deficiency, active labor
market programs, e.g., improved job matching and retraining, are needed to address structural
unemployment. Yet vocational training institutions, under the MOE, continue to graduate narrowly
trained workers using outdated pedagogy and equipment. State enterprises are more interested in
shedding than training workers and, when they do provide training, are only capable of on-the-job
training in fields for which there is declining demand. Furthermore, young adults have traditionally
been trained in a system with an excessively high proportion of overly specialized technical and
vocational secondary schools (75 percent of secondary level enrollment).
1.13   At present, both schools and labor offices lack a comprehensive, national source of caree
(occupational, educational, training, labor market) information. Career decisions are based more
upon traditional family influence and examination results than informed choice. As a result, most
youth and adults are exposed to incomplete and often inaccurate information on only a fraction of
their actual career options.
1.14  In addition, few individuals are being retrained in Romania Oess than two percent of the
unemployed in 1991 as opposed to 10-20 percent in OECD countries), and the current retraining
programs of the MOLSP are not effective. Labor office sponsored training programs have low
placement rates (about 15 percent on average) because, among other reasons, the staff cannot provide
proper screening and counseling backed up by adequate labor market analysis. With unemployment
rates which could average above 10 percent for the next five to ten years, large nmubers of workers
will need effective retraining annually. Although precise forecasts are not feasible, with the
Romanian labor force numbering about 11 million workers, training even 10 percent of the pool of
unemployed (about 1.1 million) would require an adult training system that could meet the needs of
100,000 workers each year. In addition to unemployed workers, many workers change jobs without
any or very short periods of unemployment, yet many of them also need retraining to improve their
productivity in their new jobs. If Romania is to increase its human capital stock per worker to
complement the needed expansion and renovation of its physical capital per worker, a major
restructuring of the adult training system is required. As in the case of the employment services, the
adult training system must rely upon private financing where the private rate of return is adequate and
State financing where the social returns exceed the private returns. There should also be an
appropriate balance of both private and public provision of training.
1.15 A dynamic, market-driven system of adult training, outside the monopoly control of the
Ministries of Labor and Education, administer  primarily by employer and labor representatives, is
needed. A diversified and decentralized approach is needed to develop such a system. First, some

individual training programs in existing public institutions can be revitalized, and new private training
programs encouraged, provided they meet minimum pedagogical and labor market demands. Second,
a more comprehensive overhaul of the governance and operation of exist  taining facilities should
be undertaken so that they can provide the broader range of community-based waining services needed
by adults that are common in modern market economies, but not yet available in most Romanian
institutions. It is crucial tha such a diversified approach be used becuse, while some capacity exists
for adult training, the broad institutional base for a modern adult training system does not exist in
Romania. Financial support to exist  training institutions, without a major change in govenance and
approach, or encouraging a frgmented short-term response from the private sector, will not result in
the types of adult training services needed in Romania.
D.    Social Policy Issa
1.16   Even if the issues discussed above are successfully addressed, a well functioning labor market
still may not prevent some individuals and households from falling below society's minimally
accepted standards of living and dignity - as is more likely to be the case during the period of
transition to a market economy. Thus issues of poverty alleviation, institutional weaknesses in
delivering new social safety programs, and the affordability of such programs in light of fiscal
constraints need to be addressed.
1.17              mM. The hardships inherited from decades of communism are compounded in
Romania by new problems stemming from the transition to a market economy. During the
communist era, guaante  jobs and pensions worked as a social safety net bcause many benefits
were dispensed at the workplace, although salaries and pension levels were very low. Substantial
subsidies for food, housing and transportation complemented these benefits, allowing most of the
population to avoid poverty, albeit at a fairly meager standard of living. These subsidies have now
been dismantled as part of the reform process. Furthermore, recent trends of growing unemployment
and inflation, self-employed workers outside the formal structures of social security, street children
and increased delinquency aggravate already difficult conditions for the population. The forme
system of socia protection is not nearly adequate to address these new problems. Two major groups
at risk of falling into poverty are the elderly, and households with dependent children where parents
are affected by unemployment and loss of income. To address these issues, a new approach to social
protection in Romania, which would rely more upon targeting cash assistance to families and less
upon in-kind services, must be designed and implemented.
1.18  Institutional wealcnesses. The present organizational structure of social protection is clearly
outdated for the social safety net needed in Romania today. Both social insurance and social
assistance programs have much to gain from greater harmonization, and this need for coordination
will only increase as the system develops and has a greater impact upon the lives of the Romanian
people. Problems persist with the adjudication mechanisms, overlap of some benefits, and insufficient
transparency of the system. Outreach for recipients who are enitled to benefits but don't know about
them must be improved. Too much time is spent on administrative tasks while strategic planning is
nonexistent. Much improvement can be obtained from introducing automation, strengthening policy
and evaluation capability, and implementing other organizational improvements.
4/   Cra, Iva. *Pauaw of Aduk Pautiipta in Laning Activt, Wo*w Edmio ad Tuin Poicis Puaje,
Nai=Wl Incu for Work and Leaning, WahingSt D.C., 1980.



-7-
1.19   Affordability and fiscal constraints. Ihe urgent need to reform the social protection system
must also confront fiscal realities. Romania will not be able to afford a universal social protecdon
system on the order of the French, German or Scandinavian systems. Even in these countries, fiscal
realities and labor market concerns are causing policymakers to rethink benefits and contributions
features that have long been taken for granted. Although Romania needs to move in the general
direction of the OECD systems, it must carefully review the broad spectrum of alternatives and
determine an appropriate and fiscally susainable reforms that would address immediate concerns and
start the evolution towards a viable, long-term system.
1.20 Specific issues to be addressed in the social lnsurance reform include: (a) too many funds -
there are currently at least ten funds, which are not financially viable, and impede labor mobility; (b)
improper benefit structure - in many cases, benefits are poorly related to contributions, and
discourage formal labor force participation; and (c) affordability - the tax burden from the earmarked
taxes which is too high, discouraging employment and encouraging evasion (e.g., non-payment by
small firms and the sef-employed) and a benefit structure, given long-term demographic trends,
which will be even less affordable in the future. Resources from the general budget are not likely to
be available, especially as the need for social assistance grows, implying that oveal expenditures will
have to be reduced as part of the benefit restructuring. A thorough reform of the social insurance
policy framework is essential, to be accomplished by measures to assist in its implementation through
automation of key acvities, such as contributions recordkeeping, and improvements in organizational
and financial management.
1.21  In social assistance reform, specific issues include: (a) too small and too narrow a focus -
Romania's system is oriented toward a small group of disabled or handicapped people and is not
prepared to help households lift themselves out of poverty; (b) confused legal framework - a flood of
laws and regulations have been issued in response to the imminent increae of the destitute and to
respond to pressure groups; these have simply added to the benefits existing from the Ceausescu
years, without priorities being established; (c) fragmentation of services throughout many agencies -
there is a large number of agencies involved in the delivery of social assistance, creating coordination
difficulties, spreading the benefits into small amounts, and producing inefficiency; (d) unused
entitlements and lack of community outreach - even for approved benefits many potential beneficiaries
are not receiving them for lack of a deliberate outreach effort towards those in need; (e) data
inadequacies - Romania is forced to design a system in a vacuum, as the lack of random sample
comprehensive household survey data leaves Romania with no way to analyze who are the
poor/disadvantaged, what are their needs, and how can these needs be met; and (f) shortage of skills
and institutional capacity - the shortage of trained social workers, social program managers, and
social scientists is an impediment to designing and implementing reform programs.
E.    Govemnment Sector Policies and Rationale for Bank Assistance
1.22 The Government is concerned about protecting the living standards of the population as the
transition proceeds, yet, at the same time, it recognizes the severe fiscal constraints that it faces. To
assist the Government in formulating a strategy for addressing these issues, the Bank undertook sector



work with the Government in late 1990, which culminated in a red cover sector report -'. This
report laid out the recommended policy reforms for all of the social sectors and provided the sectoral
strategy for a sequence of Bank operations to assist those sectors. The rationale underlying the sector
strategy is to focus, first, on the most basic services which are critical for the short- to medium-term
social safety net and, then, on those services that are essential for a sustainable transition to a market
economy. Although the standard public economics arguments, such as social externalities or capital
market failures, apply to the social sectors, the sector strategy recognizes that there still should be an
appropriate mix of private and public finance and provision of servces.
1.23 A Letter of Development Policy (LDP), signed jointly by the Ministers of State for Finance
and for Labor and Social Protection, was sent to the Bank in October 1993 (see Annex 3 for the text
of this letter). The LDP oudines the overall policy framework the Government intends to pursue with
regard to social safety net issues. In addition to the LDP, the MOLSP, as the Government's lead
agency in the provision of a social safety net, has completed two substantia White Papers, one on
social assistance and the other on social insurance. These White Papers, which spell out the
Government's specific reform program in these areas, were prepared during the past year with full
pardcipation of MOLSP top management and professional staff, announced in press conferences, and
then published for public discussion and comment prior to formulating legislation. The Socia
Assistance White Paper (see Annex 4 for an executive summary) introduces a new system of cash
benefits focused on those in deepest poverty and most likely to suffer economic hardships during the
trasition. The Social Insurance White Paper (see Annex S for an executive summary) focuses on
unifying the many different pension funds, raising the retirement age, introducing better financial
management of social insurance funds, and gradually developing a private pension system to
complement the public one. These white papers were well received and generated significant
attention in the press. Given the repressive policies of the Ceausescu regime, this open discussion
marks a significant change in Romanian social policy and has fostered strong Government ownership
of the social policy strategy being pursued.
1.24  In line with the sector stategy, the first project activity in the sector augmented the capacity
of the Government to set up a targeted social safety net by supporti  the itroduction of critical
programs (such as unemployment benefits) through the Employment Component of the Technical
Assistance and Critical Imports Project (TACI, Loan 3363-RO, FY91). The project would follow up
on these initial efforts of the TACI Project by supporting the implementation of far-reaching policy
reforms. A Health Rehabilitation Project (Loan 3409-RO, FY91) followed soon after the TACI
Project, aiming to address the serious crises in the public health system as a result of years of
underinvestment and mismanagement. In the area of education, the Education Reform Project (Loan
3724-RO, FY94) supports the reform of educational curricula, teacher training, textbook provision
(including competitive bidding by textbook firms), and improved management and financing of basic
education. In a parallel project, financing for a major component (prepared and appraised as an
integral component of this project) to reform provision of secondary and vocational training within the
formal education system is being provided by the European Union. Finally, development of private
universities and the reform of public universities and research institutes are the subject of a strategy
paper being prepared by the Government. A project to implement these reforms will be identified on

the basis of the Bank's dialogue with the Government concerning this higher education and research
strategy paper. Together, these operations, as well as underlying sector work and policy dialogue and
development, constitute a coherent, if parsimonious, initial approach to overall sector reform in
Romania.
1.25   The Bank has now accumulated substantial social sector project experience in Romania, and a
number of lessons have been learned as result. Those lessons have been reflected in the design of the
project. First, the inexperience of project agency staff in procurement and disbursement practices of
the Bank requires extraordinary education and training efforts on the part of Bank staff to ensure an
adequate working inowledge in implementing agencies. Second, the generally weak public
administration capacity of the social sector ministries causes both preparation and implementation
problems. Third, the planning and budgeting methods of the Ministry of Finance and the sectoral
ministries have serious rigidities which need to be taken into account to ensure the tidmely flow of
local counterpart funds to project activities. To address these issues the Project Coordination Unit
(PCU), currently under the TACI Project, will be expanded and more intensive training in
procurement and other aspects of implementation will be provided to PCU staff. In addition,
coordination between the MOF and the MOLSP has been fostered by insisting that the Government
financing plan for all activities supported by the project be prepared jointly by these two agencies to
ensure commitment and clear understanding of financing requirements.

H. THEPROQiCr
A.    Project Objectives
2.1   The project will support the Govennment's eflorts to alleviate unemployment and poverty,
improve the efficiency and affordability of employment and social protection services, and promote
labor productivity and economic competitiveness. The specific objectives of the project are to:
(a) strengthen the capacity of labor offices to administ increasing numbers of claims for
unemployment benefits and active labor adjustment services (e.g., job placement, employment
counseling, training); (b) develop a flexible adult training system which responds to evolving labor
market demand resulting from economic restructuring; and (c) implement reforms in social insurance
and assistance programs, targeting those population groups which are most vulnerable to falling into
poverty during and after the transition to a market economy.
B.    Project DescriRtioo
2.2   The project will be implemented over five years by the Ministry of Labor and Social
Protection (MOLSP). Total project cost is estimated at US$95.2 million including contingencies, of
which US$81.6 million represents base cost. The project will finance civil works, equipment and
materials, vehicles, computer hardware and software, technical assistance, external and local training,
and incremental operating costs. It comprises three interrelated components structured as follows:
I.   General Management and Policy
A. Monitoring and Policy Analysis
B. Automation
C. Project Management
II.   Employment and Training
A. Employment Services
B. Career Information and Counseling
C. Adult Training
m.   Social Protection
A. Social Insurance
B. Social Assistance



-11-
Component I: General Manageument and Policy l
2.3    The overall objective of this component is to improve management, planning, execution, cost-
effectiveness, and evaluation of MOLSP social programs.
2.4    Monitoring and Policy Analysis (USS3.9 million). Under this subcomponent, the project will
support the development of a Management I#onnation System (MIS) to track and evaluate ongoing
programs and help refine new policies introduced under the project. Ihese evaluation procedures,
based upon the analysis of administratve data generated by the MIS, will be tested in at least two
judets, and then introduced in the other judets for evaluating, planning and budgeting for local
employment and social assistance programs. Guidelines for integrating data collection and evaluation
procedures into the MIS will be finalized, materials developed, and at least one labor office staff
person in each judet trained in Integrating these procedures into local operations.
2.5    An applied research and evaluation program will be developed under this subcomponent to
complement administrative data obtained from the MIS. This program will be based on special
household and establishment surveys necessary to obtain information on persons not currently
participating in MOLSP programs, thereby establishing appropriate control groups necessary to
evaluate overall program effectiveness. The concerned MOLSP departments will together decide
upon the aspects of program effectiveness to be investigated, plan and carry out the sample surveys
(the MOLSP will work with the National Statistical Commission, or with a private survey firm, to
execute the survey field work), and analyze and publish results. In addition to the special surveys, a
second round of Integrated Household Surveys (the first round is being conducted under the ongoing
TACI Project) will be carried out with a view to monitoring trends in nutrition status and other living
standards in the aggregate, as well as for the bottom deciles of the population.
2.6    Finally, a data collection system for occupational sqfety will be developed.-' In the past,
rather than directly addressing the real causes of unsafe work conditions, workers were paid extra
allowances and bonuses as incentives for taking up hazardous occupations. Workcplace accident
statistics were deliberately underreported, making it difficult to design appropriate program
interventions. The project will support improvements in the collection of occupational safety
statistics, including studies of ways to initiate low-cost interventions aiming to reduce accidents and
contribute to improved productivity. It is expected that some of these initiatives could then be
incorporated into the adult training programs under the Employment and Training Component
(described in paras. 2.14-2.34).

2.8    Automation (IbSS21.2 million). The automation subcomponet will help to improve the

introducing automation of a number of activities, such as the contributions records system of social
insurance, and the unemployment benefit system. A long-term strategic plan comprising three phase
has been drafted by the MOLSP to serve as the framework for this undertaking. Phase I, involving
installation of PCs in the main labor and social  istance offices, is nearing completion; this phase
has been supported by the ongoing TACI Project. The subcomponent in the current project will
support Phase U, which aims to increase the availability and use of information technology through
the use of Local Area Networks (LANs), and mid-range computers for larger data bases. 2/
2.9    Also as a part of Phase I activities supported by the project, organizational changes to better
manage information technology will be implemented. An Information Resources Management
Directorate (RMD) will be established in the MOLSP to be responsible for managing, planning, and
coordinating automation activities. It will also provide general administrative support in the Ministry
for financial management, budgeting, planning, personnel, payroll, procurement, and property and
inventory management.
2.10   Specific activities to be supported under the automation subcomponent are:
(a)    refinement of the long-term strategic plan and short-term action plans to implement
strategic objectives; definition of functional requirements, specifications of hardware
and software for Phase II; coordination with end-users in other subcomponents of the
project; and procurement of equipment and services;
(b)    design and testing of software applications (e.g., for unemployment benefits, social
assistance benefits and pensions), and development and implementation of software
and user training; and
(c)    installation of computers and reated hardware, including the establishment of the
National Data Center equipped with a mainframe and, for the judets, mid-range
computers with a mix of terminals and PCs.
2.11   The project will finance, in addition to hardware and software requirements, incremental
operating costs, and the necessary rehabilitation of buildings to prepare them for hardware
installation. All of the 170 offices labor, social assistance and social insurance) of the MOLSP will

Component I: 1EnDLme  and Training
2.13   The overall objective of this component is to strengthen the capacity of the MOLSP to
administer unemployment benefits and pro-active labor programs, and to develop a flexible and
responsive adult training system.
2.14 Employment Services (USS18.8 million). Ibis subcomponent aims to improve the capacity of
the MOLSP's Employment Services to: (a) deliver income support to the unemployed through the
Unemployment Benefit (UB) system; (b) assist in the rapid redeployment of excess labor; and
(c) implement active labor programs to upgrade human capital. These objectives will be achieved
through three parallel activities: the first focusing on refining national administration and policy; the
second on development and delivery of services at local employment offices; and the third on
development of special procedures to deal with large-scale layoffs.
2.15   National Admrdstrtlon and Poicy. This first set of activities will review and revise the
national management structure of MOLSP's Employment Services and the ways in which labor
market policy is formulated and implemented. Special emphasis will be placed on improving:
(a) overall cost-effectiveness of employment service administration, including increased automation
with sufficient flexibility to adapt to future modifications in employment service programs;
(b) delivery of unemployment benefits (UB) in conjunction with Social Security and SocW Assistance;
(c) establishment of a national labor exchange; and (d) administration of active labor adjustment
programs. Revised Employment Services management structures will be defined and adopted, and
supporting legislation and regulations developed as necessary. In addition, taining will be provided
to national and local personnel in job counseling, job development, etc. With respect to labor market
policy, special studies (i.e., on labor mobility, women's employment, impact of restructuring on
employment) will be carried out to enable the MOLSP to refine its labor policy, including
implementing the results of the labor market policy evaluations in the first component (para. 2.5).
Based on the recommendations of these studies as well as experience gained, a plan will be developed
to refine selected elements of labor market policies for the period 1995 - 1998.
2.16   Local Labor Offices. The second set of activities under this subcomponent will support the
development of work plans and related staff training materials to: (a) strengthen the management
capacity of labor offices; (b) expand the labor exchange function to include active labor adjustment
measures; (c) implement refinements in the operation of the UB program, in part through increased
automation; (d) improve client intake, registration and job placement services; and (e) improve job
development programs by increasing job vacancy listings, utilizing qualitative and quantitative

2.28 The NTB will support adult training programs for workers that rely upon two modes of
delivery: (i) programs offered by newly established Regional Adult Training Centers (RATCs) (see
below); and (ii) contract training provided by private and public institutions slected competitively on
a program-by-program basis. In order to encourage flexibility and relevance in the provision of adult
training, all NTB-suprted programs will meet the folowing criteria: evdence of labor market
demand; agreement to serve the needs of a varied clientele (e.g., out of school youth, employed and
unmployed adults); minimm service delivery criteria for training programs (e.g. flexible entry/exit
programs); and fincing criteria (e.g., normative funding based on number of full-ime equivalent
trainees). In addition, to be eligible for NTB support, RATCs will have to agree to provide m
support services (e.g., intake counseling, general and vocational skills, custom training for
enterprises, small enterprise training, and placemnt services); and meet other minimum criteria to be
selected as a developmental site. Unemployed workers, after receiving career information and job
search assistance, will thus be able to choose among a range of training services supplied by both
private and public institutions, thereby expressing effiective demand for the type of training which best
helps them in the labor market.
2.29   Financing of adult training will be based on the following principles: All project investment
resources allocated for local insdtutions/sites, individual adult training programs wiUl be controlled by
the NTB and disbursed according to criteria and procedures agreed with the Bank. Recurrent coss
for the small contingent of NTB staff will be covered from the budget. Recurrent costs (i.e., unit
costs of training) will be financed from multiple sources which will vary depending on the instittion
providing the service and client group services. Budget resources will finance training the long-term
unemployed or school leavers for which there is public good justification. Budget resources will aso
be used to pardally offset the costs of retraining employed workers in recognition that this is an
investment in human capital with positive social externalities. Fees or user charges paid by employed
individuals and firms, will be used to offset all or a major part (i.e., 50 percent) of the costs of
training employed workers at all institutions (e.g., RATCs and individual adult training programs).
2.30   Reioa A    Training Centers (RATC). On the basis of proposals submitted by CTBs to
the NTB, RATCs wiUl be established to provide comprehensive, community-based training services
that are responsive to local labor market needs. The project will support the establishment of three
RATCs. RATCs proposals must be from local authorities and indicate that: (a) the RATC will be an
independent agency; (b) it will be under the authority of a tripartite Regional ocal) Training Board
composed of rqresentatives of Employers, Workers and Government with each group having equal
voice; and (c) it will be independent of national government agencies but operated in accord with
policies of the National Training Board and applicable laws. In order to ensure that the training needs
of the emerging private sector are adequately represented, the private sector share of the employers'
representation on the CTBs wil be at minimum 50 percent except in those few instances where local
private sector development and representation are embryonic. Furthermore, a subcommittee of the
CTBs will be formed to determine the specific skills for which training will be supported by the
CTBs. To ensure adequate private sector representation in deermining skill needs, at least 75 percen
of the membership of this committee will be from the private sector. Three similar training centers
have already been established in Romania as independent local agencies with German bilateral
assistance and are operating successfully. The RATC will be st up in existn fcilities idntified by
each community - sometimes including using the physical facilities of firmer vocational secondary
schools in accord with legal provisions for transfer and use of such property - and will provide sbort-
and medium-term occupational training reflecting actual labor market needs for both the omployed
and unemployed. The centers, each having a capacity of 2,000 traines anually, will serve both

paricular emerging small- and medium-sized enterprises which need acces to taining for dhir
wodrkrs. (See part A of Annex SA for more details on the operations of RATCs.)

2.43   Social Assistance (USS5.3 million). The objective of this subcomponent is to implement the
new cash benefits law and resolve a number of problems that presently hamper efficient delivery of
social assistance, including: too many untargeted benefits, fragmentation of services among many
agencies, management and operational problems, lack of socioeconomic data and evaluation, reduced
coverage and unmet needs of entitled benefits, mixed funding of benefits from central and local
budgets resulting in unclear assignment of responsibility, and lack of policy-malking capability. To
address these issues, three main areas will be supported under the project.
2.44  The poUcy refonn required to consolidate the current fragmented system and change the focus
from vulnerable individuals to households with insufficient income will be supported in three ways:
(i) based upon reforms recommended in the Social Assistance White Paper (see para 1.23), which was
reviewed by the Bank during the July 1993 appraisal mission, a new system will be proposed that is
consistent with the new economic and polidcal environment, and takes into consideration the issues
raised above; (ii) the replacement of the existng, fragmented legislation by an integrated law on
social assistance; and (iii) organizational reform in the MOLSP that reflecs the changes needed to
implement the new legislation.
2.45   As a conditon of dwisuEmen on the social assistance subcomponent, the Government
agreed that withdrawals shal be subject to the enactment of a Social Assistance law, reflecting the
principles set forth in the Social Assistance White Paper (previously finalized by the Govermt and
agreed with the Bank), and the completion of the implementing arrangements for the law.
2.46  Improved twgenAg and  eliaon of servica will be supported through the elaboration
of regulations governing the actual implementation of the cash benefit program. Improvements will
be made in the social inquiry, which is based upon home visits by social workers for households
needing pro-active social work. Decentralization of activities will be achieved through alterative
means of delivering some services, e.g., by NOs and community groups. Closer links will be
forged between MOLSP, Local Councils, NGOs and local community groups to increase their
awareness of social assistance and its importance, and to encourage their participation in information
exchange within the community.

(c) ForeigM Exchge Comonen. The foreign exchange component was estimated as
follows: (i) civil works - 35 percent; (ii) equipment and materials, vehicles and
computers - 80 percent; (iii) experts from abroad - 80 percent; (iv) experts recruited

(d) Customs Duties and Taxes. A Value Added Tax (VAT) of 18 percent was introduced
in July 1993. This tax has been applied to all goods fianced under the project, as has
a 5 percent customs duty. Total taxes and duties included in the project cost amount
to US$8.7 million. Payment of taxes and duties will be assured by the Government
through the budget approved for the MOLSP.
3.3       Re      t Costs. Project cost estimates include only incremental operating costs of the
investment. Appraisal estimates indicate that total incremental operating costs during the Project will
be about US$20.4 million, of which US$12.7 million for local staff salaries, and the remainder for
supplies and other expenses related to project management (e.g., vehicle and equipment maintenance)
incurred during implementation. The Government will finance all incremental recurrent costs, with
the exception of those for supplies, which will be financed under the Bank loan (estimated US$7.6
million).
3.4      Implementing the policy and program reforms required to establish the social safety net
under this project will entail significant incremental recurrent expenditures. These expenditures will
cover additional required staff (included in the project costs) to deliver the employment and social
asstce services to support dislocated workers and families. Table 3.3 below gives a more detailed
cross-tabulation of expenditure categories for each component. As shown there, the recurrent costs
for local staff salaries in the Employment Services Component and, to a lesser degree, incremental
staffing in several other components, are the main sources of the essential incremental recurrent costs.
The required staffing of the Employment Services is estimated to increase from 2,000 to 6,000 by the
end of the project in order to reduce the client:staff ratio from over 650:1 to 250:1 (considered to be
a barely acceptable level) during a time of rising unemployment. After the completion of the project,
when unemployment settles down to more normal levels, then the Employment Services will have an
adequate staffing ratio of about 125:1.
3.5       The budgetary costs of the social insurance and assistance benefit payments are not
included in project costs since these are neither investment nor recurrent costs, but represent transfer
payments for social protection. Detailed actuarial projections and analysis of the proposed social
insurance reforms were carried out during preparation of the Social Insurance White Paper by the
MOLSP and the Harvard Institute for International Development (HUID). The results of these
projections and analysis show that the State Insurance Fund (SIF) will remain in balance, or at a
slight surplus, as a result of reforms to increase the retirement age, thereby: (i) reducing the number
of beneficiaries from 3.2 million in 1995 to 2.7 million by 2010, and (ii) increasing the number of
contbutors from 6.5 million to 7.6 million over the same time period. The ratio of beneficiaries to
contnutors (dependency ratio) will decline significantly from 0.49 in 1995 to 0.36 in 2010. With
the SIF remaining in balance or at a slight surplus, it will not be necessary to cover the SIF
expenditures from the State budget. However, the public budget will be the source of financing for
new cash benefits targeting households in deepest poverty. The reforms will limit the eligibility
criteria and the level of benefits so that the total cash benefits programs amount to less than one
percent of GDP. Thus the social insurance and assistance transfer payments will not constitute an
undue fiscal burden.



-25-
3.6         The incremental staffing costs of the project will average about US$2.1 milion per year
over the life of the project (approximady the same as the staff costs of the MOLSP in 1992).
However, this amount represents only about 1.8 percent of the transfer budget, such as unemployment
benefits and pensions payments, which will be administered by these staff. The Government has
agreed with the Bank and the Fund to reduce budget subsidies in other areas, which in turn will make
the incremental recurrent costs of this project sustainable. In addition, cost recovery in the form of
antaprises paying a share of the trainng cost for their workers will also enhance sustainabiity.

C.          Prject Manament and IMRIementation
3.8         Project Manaumme   Strutr.   The project management structure will comprise:
(a) Technical Departments - responsible for professional and technical implementation of their
respective subcomponents as an integral part of their professional responsibilities; (b) the PCU -



-28-
responsible for administrative support in areas such as procurement and arrangements for TA; and
(c) a Technical Coordinating Committee (TCC) - responsible for coordinating implementation among
subcomponents as required and monitoring progress towards attainment of project objectives, policy
measures, etc. The general outline of the roles of the TDs for each subcomponent is given below,
followed by the roles of the PCU and the TCC (see Chart 3.1 for a listing of the implementing
departments and the Project Operating Manual for more detail on these roles).
3.9         Monitoring and Evaluation Subcomponent. A Monitoring and Evaluation Group (MEG)
will coordinate the implementation of this component. The Department of Social Idicators will play
the lead role in the MEG, which will bring together the various departments of the MOLSP that are
involved in the project. A significant part of the monitoring data will be produced through the MIS,
but there also will be the special purpose surveys to collect additional types of data. The MEG will
focus on oversight of the evaluation and publication of these data sources for policy purposes.
3.10        Automation Subcompnent. The Information Resources Management Directorate (IRMD)
will implement this component in close coordination with the end users whose needs are to be served.
A strategic plan for the IRMD has been prepared and will guide the implementation strategy for the
automation of IRMD. A new director and staff have been appointed for the IRMD. Training in
procurement of computers has already started for the IRMD staff, who already have some experience
in this area from the TACI Project and the Japan Grants.

3.11           Emplo=ment Services SubcomRonent. Implementation of this subcomponent will be
coordinated by the Secretary of State for Employment, Division of Employment, and will require
identification and allocation of at least seven fill-time staff to deal with the national and local
employment service activities. In addition a field service staff (approximately 16) will be attached to
an agency selected by MOLSP, in consultation with the NTB, to provide leadership for development



-29-
of Industrial Adjustment Services at the national and local levels. Technical asistance, including
foreign/local experts and related foreign/local training, will be delivered by one major contractor
according to terms of reference agreed with the Government and the Bank.
3.12       The TA contractor will enter into a sub-contractual relationship with a local agency and
will be responsible for delivering the large number of training programs to about 5000 local labor
office staff and 16 industrial adjustment staff once procedures, materials, and training programs will
have been developed. The TA contract will cover Romanian instructors, including travel and per
diem costs for the 5,000 local labor office staff who will each receive up to one month of training
over the duration of the project.
3.13        Career Information and Counseling Subcomoonent. Implementation of this
subcomponent will be coordinated by the Secretary of State for Employment, Division of
Employment. Activities will be implemented by the CICWG, staffed by about ten full-time
professional staff and three secretarial and clerical support personnel. It will be assisted in this task
by a broad-based Career Information and Counseling Advisory Group (CICAG) which will meet at
least twice annually to review CICWG plans and products, and make recommendations and taWe
actions to ensure they meet the needs of the diverse population of consumers of its career information
and counseling products. CICAG members will also facilitate local pilot testing, evaluation and
validation of materials and procedures developed by the CICWG. The CICWG will be located at the
Institute of Education Sciences, a site which is mutually agreeable to the MOLSP, Ministry of Youth
and Sports, and the Ministry of Education. Technical assistance (foreign consultants and fellowships)
will be provided through parallel Canadian Government Financing, in accordance with terms of
reference agreed upon by the Bank and MOLSP. The project will provide supplemental financing for
local consultants and training to be administered by the Division of Employment in coordination with
the Terms of Reference agreed between MOLSP, the Bank and the Canadian Government.
3.14        Adult Trainine Subcomn t. Implementation will be coordinated by the Secretary of
State for Employment, Division of Training, in cooperation with the National Training Board (NTB)
and its staff. Both adult and management training activities will be administered by the staff of the
NTB located in the MOLSP. These staff will be administratively attached to the MOLSP, funded
through the State budget or the Employment Fund, and will include approximately 13 professional
and four support personnel to cover the following functions: coordination and administration,
vocational training, general education, entrepreneurship, civil works, finance, occupational
counseling, enterprise services, evaluation and assessment. The Director, appointed by the MOLSP,
will be subject to the endorsement of the NTB.
3.15        Technical assistance (foreign/local consultants and foreign/local training) under this
subcomponent will be obtained from one general contractor according to TORs agreed with the
Government and the Bank. The contractor will provide technical assistance for the detailed
development of three RATCs and individual programs in existing and new private and public training
institutions. In addition, the NTB will be supported by German Bilateral Assistance (interational and
local consultants). The project will supplement this support by providing for fellowships which will
be directly administered by the NTB. The NTB will be responsible for initiating the process of
selection of eligible beneficiaries which will receive project resources (RATCs and individual adult
training programs) following criteria agreed with the Bank. Each individual project will be presented
for approval to the NTB by the NTB staff. Beneficiaries will sign contracts with the NTB/MOLSP
following which project resources will be made available to beneficiaries.



-30-
3.16        Social Insurance Subcomgonent. Implementation of this subcomponent will be carried
out by the newly formed Social Insurance Organization (SIO), which will be under the general policy
direction of the MOLSP. The TA contract (see POM for the detailed TOR #8) will provide expert
assistance to the MOLSP and the SIO during the transitional process during which the detailed
organization plan of the SIO is to be formulated. Many of the staff currently administering social
insurance will be transferred from Central Office for Pension Payments (COPP) to the SIO, but a few
will remain in the MOLSP to perform a policy and advisory role. The extensive automation
requirements for the newly reformed pension system will be met in collaboration with the IRMD
according to the TOR #2 in the automation component of this project.
3.17        Social Assistance Subcow onent. The Directorate for Social Assistance (DSA) within
the MOLSP will implement this component. The DSA supervises the Judet level social assistance
offices which will have a key role in implementing the new cash benefits and social service delivery.
Both the central and judet level offices will receive technical assistance and training according to
TOR#9. The DSA will cooperate with the local mayors' offices in the adminisration of the new cash
benefits programs and with the local education authorities (for those children enrolled in schools) in
the administration of child allowances. The TA contractors will assist the DSA and judet level social
assistance offices in developing its MIS and office work procedures to take maximum advantage of
the automation support to be introduced by the IRMD.
3.18        Project Coordination Unit. A Project Coordination Unit was established within the
MOLSP in September 1991 to coordinate the implementation of the Employment Component of the
TACI Project. This PCU has performed reasonably well, given that this was its first experience in
implementing a Bank project. Based upon the lessons learned in implementing the TACI Project and
preparing this project, the PCU is being expanded from two to six full-time staff to carry out a more
clearly defined role in the management structure of the project. The expanded PCU will execute the
decisions taken by the TCC, and will also provide the TDs with support in project management,
procurement and reporting. The PCU will perform, Ler alia, the following two specific functions:
(a)    assist the various TDs with such technical tasks as project accounts, procurement,
payments, establishment of contracts, communications with the Bank, etc.; and
(b)    monitor and report to MOLSP management and the Bank on the progress toward
achievement of the project objectives and the agreed policy measures.
3.19        Durine negotiations, the Government confirmed that the PCU staff has been expanded
to at least six full-time staff, including one person with a strong project management background, one
general administrator, one procurement specialist, one accountant, one bilingual secretary and one
translator. A condition of effectiveness is that the TA contractor for the PCU has been selected.
3.20        T                              A Technical Coordinating Committee comprising
representatives of each of the TDs will be established to coordinate the implementation of the project.
Each TD and the PCU will appoint one of its members to sit on the TCC. A scretary and
spokesperson will be selected for the committee. This committee will deliberate monthly on all
matters affecting the project and report directly to the Minister.
3.21        Poect Supervision. Supervision will need to include, in addition to the task manager
and project implementation specialist, periodic visits by specialists in automation, employment and



-31-
training, social insurance and social assistance. To help ensure smooth implementation, provide
trouble-shooting, and economize on supervision resources, the Human Resources Projects Officer
Oocal staff) of the Bucharest Resident Mission will provide support and follow-up with the MOLSP as
necessary.

will not receive project funds to undertake procurement, but will be involved in developing equipment
lists and technical specifications, and evaluating bids).
