SOCIAL INSURANCE WHITE PAPER
EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
Social policies and social protection are integral parts of Romania's reform strategy, closely
lined to the economic and political developments necessary to support the restuturing of
the national economy.
The social program of the Government of Romania for 1993-96 takes into account not
only immediate demands but also future needs, projected on the basis of likely demographic
trends and the prospects for technological development and economic growth. In developing
the program and setting goals for social policy and social protection, the Government took as
its starting point the provisions of the Romanian constitution addressing social needs and
risks and undamental human rights, and a number of other principles set forth in
international regulations and recommendations.
Between 1990 and 1992 the Govemment concentrated mainly on short-term measures
that were reparative or intended to keep social programs operating at a certain level. But
since 1992-and particularly since 1993-the Government has directed its efforts toward.
longer-term goals.
Social protection, defined as the entire set of actions taken by society to prevent
alleviate, or eliminate the effects on living standards of 'scial risks," is important in
democratic societies and a priority in the Government's social sector reform strateg. .he
social insurance and pension system-the main element of social procection in Romana-and
its problems have been a constant concern of the Ministry of Labor and Social Protection.
Through a wide range of measures aimed at making the system function better, redressing
past wrongs, and ensuring that the system remains financially solvcrt without being
essentially changed, the ministry demonstrated its principles for coverge under the system
and the system's planned organizational and administrative structur.
Romania began its transition to a market economy almost four years ago, undergoing
a painful, comprehensive process of reform during which social developments and social



Annex 5
Page 2 of 8
costs have often been unpredictable. Now the process of reform must start in tie social
security and pension system, too, to ensure that over the medium and long term the system is
organized and run according to the new principles Romania is adopting.
A fir step was to daft and publish this white paper on the social insuce sysm
of Romania, consistent with the Economic and Social Reform Strategy of the Government
Program for 1993-96. This paper challenges the public appraisal and discussion-at all
levels-of the proposed reforms of the social security and pension systm, with the aim of
contibuting to the proposals and supportig the daftig of the necesry laws and the
purposeful, well-grounded implementation of the new system. The paper makes
recommendations for the reform of Romania's social protection system-one of the world's
most comprehensive-and seeks to substatiate them through analysis of the evolution of
social security systems in other countries and the development of Romania's own social
insurance and pension system.
The introductory chapter briefly surveys the histoxy of social security systems from
their modem beginnings in Chancellor Bismarck's Germany to their development and
improvement in the post-World War II Britain of William Beveridge. The purpose of the
survey is to trace the evolution of the concepts social security and social insurmce as they
are currently understood by the international community.
A survey classifying the stages-from young to mature-that redistbutive social
insurance systems pass through places Romania's among the mature systems. Ihis
classification scheme is helpful not only because it highlights the predictable course of
different kinds of systems but also because Romania can learn lessons from other countris in
its group. An important lesson of the survey is that short- and medium-term measures to
reform the social insurne and pension system must be initiated now in order to avoid the
painful situations currendy confronting industial countries with mature systems. In fact, a
picture of the reforms-completed or in progress-in the industrial counties as well as in
Central and East Europen countries shows that the reform of Romania's social insurance
and pension system is necessary not only because of the country's transition from a cenally



87                            Annex S
Page 3 of 8
planned to a market economy, but also because of sociodemographic developments, the need
for steadily balanced finances, and the goal of achieving fair distnbution between and within
generations over the medium and long term.
Chapter 2 provides detailed information on the evolution of Romania's social
insurance and pension system from its beginnings to the present day and on the system's
current organization and administration. In descnbing the main goals and characteristics of
the system as it passed through different stages of development, the chapter emphasizes the
period 1944-89, comparing it with the period since 1989, when significant changes were
made to the system to sustain it during the early stages of the transition to a market
economy.
The chapter discusses the social insurance and pension system in detail, focusing on
its legal and organizational framework and on the sources of revenue for its components,
which include the state social insurance system, farmers' social insurance system,
supplementary pension system, and independent social insurance systems. It also gives a
detailed description of the social insurance benefits, from base pensions under the state
system to noncontributory benefits with a reparative element that have been granted to
Revolution heroes or wounded, or to people persecuted under the communist dictatorship for
their political beliefs.
To give an idea of the place and role of the social insurance and pension system in
the macroeconomy, the chapter gives the expenditure on different benefits as a share of gross
domestic product (GDP), along with the trends over the past 12 years and projections for the
future. These figures are contrasted with pensions as a percentage of GDP for couraras of
the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD). Chapter 2 rds &with
a brief analysis of the main expenditure items of Romania's state pension system, its most
representative social insurance and pension system.
Efficient social protection should be closely linked with the development of Romania's
economic system. It is for this reason that chapter 3 addresses the Romania economy.
Starting with a general consideration of the stabilization and macroeconomic reform efforts



88
Annex S
Page 4 of 8
that Romania began in 1991 during an eonomic slump, the chapter surveys macroecono
developments over the past few years and assesses their impact It briefly analyzes the
structural reforms that were or are bein  mplemented-liberalization of prices and trade,
refrm of the banking system, r        and privtion of entrpris-to relate the
proposed social msurnce and pension system reforms to economic developments. The
anlys highlights a number of sectoral problems as well as their plamed solutions under the
Govnmu's economic and social reform strategy.
The chapter also gives forecat of macroeconom  developments over the medium
term; these predict a positive growth in the productive sectr beginning in 1995, bringing
wit it a rise in exports and a decline in the budget deficit to about I percet of GDP by the
end of the decade. These macreonomc projections allow a more realistic assessment of the
prospets for succes of Romania's proposed social insurance and pension system reforms
and help in judging how the reforms should be paced.
Chapter 4 reviews Romania's curent social insurance and pension system to identify
the organzational and operationl aspects that should be reformed during and after the
transition to a market economy. The main conclusions of the review are as follows:
0-ne present system is far too diversified and complex. The existence of several
independent systems plus the supplemey pension scheme incurs avoidable administrative
costs and results in inequitable treatment of pensioners. The multiplicity of pension schemes
also hinders the movement of labor between locations and even between occupations; such
mobility is essental for the proper fucioning of a market economy.
* In the light of Romania's present and fuure economic conditions and projected
demographic trends, the country's low standard retrement ages cannot be justified. Its
retrement ages are below those in the vast majority of the industial economies, which have
been compelled to rise them. Nor can the lower retrement age for women than for men be
just}fied. Also adding avoidable costs in pensions-and wasti  potential productive
resources-is the lack of services for rehabilitation and retraining of the disabled.



89                             Annx S
Page 5 of 8
* The current method of calculating pensions has several shortcomings. The practice
of limiting the camnings base used in the calclation to the income eamed in the five years
prior to retirement invites abuse, and the potential for such abuse will increase as small
private frms increse in mmber. At the same time, the absence of a ceiling on pensionable
income can lead m a transitonal economy to large variations in the sze of public pensions.
And the decreasing annual accrual factors used after 15 years of service crate a disincentive
to continue working beyond the standard retirement age.
* Under the current rapid inflation, the lack of automatic indexation of pensions
leaves pensioners' income dependent on administative discretion.
* The tax exemption of all pensions favors those receivirR large pensions and
provides an incentive for early retirement.
* That employers pay most of the social insurance contribution, with employees
contributing only 3 percent to the supplementary pension fund, crates an impression that
pensions are an obligation of the state, not a benefit to which people are entitled because of
the contributions they make during their working lives.
- Those responsible for preparing and implementing the social insurmnce budget lack
modem data-processing equipment and up-to-date information, both of which are esentl for
flexible decisionmaking in an unstable economic climate.
X The supplementary pension system, now regarded as an extension of the state
pension system, no longer fulfills its original function as an instument to insure its
members' personal income. Because of rapid inflation and a decision to extend a credit to the
farmers' pension system, the supplementary pension fund has been almost entirely
decapitalized before it could reach maturity.
* Temporary disability benefits, because they are tax-free, can provide employees
eligible for the highest benefit rates (those with more than eight years of service) higher
incomes during medical leave than when they report to work, creating a disincentive for
working. The four different benefit rates for medical leave, which corespond to the length
of employees' service, cannot be justified.



90                            Armez 5
Page 6 of 8
0 The current organizational and administdve Systems for social insurance and
pension benefits are poorly designed, with administative and policy functions inadequatly
differentiated. The systems are also underfunded. The administrative workload has increased
dramatically with the rapid economic shifts and changes in peswion benefits and eligibility,
but the greater workload has not been matched by growth in staff or an improvement in staff
skills. Moreover, the administrative system lacks data-processing equipment and adequate
space for offices and archives.
* An analysis of medium- and long-term conditions under the present social insrace
and pension system of Romania in the light of the most likely social and demographic trends
points to a worsening dependency ratio (the ratio of beneficiaries to contributors). Assuming
that the wage replacement rate remains at about 60 percent, social inrance contributions
would have to be increased to rates far above the highest present rates and hardly acceptable
to the active population. Conversely, if social insurance contnbutions were kept at current
rates, the wage replacement rate would drop significantly, an intolerable outcome for
pensioners.
Chapter 5 outlines the proposed reforms of Romania's social insuance and pension
system. These reforms have been designed with the aim of creating a more coherent system
and one more compatible with the social and economic system that the country seeks to
build.
After describing the main objectives of the new social insurance system, and of the
pension system in particular, the chapter discusses the system's proposed organizational and
functional structure; its benefits structure, eligibility criteria, and method of calculating
benefits; the new system's funding and costs over the medium and long term; and a strategy
for developing a complementary voluntary private pension system.
The new organizational and funcdonal strcture implies a unified public pension
system that would integrate all the independent systems, including the farmers' scial
insurance and pension system and the supplementary scheme. Administrative responsibility



91                             _ne 5
Page 7 of 8
for the system would be transferred gradually to a new, independent agency, with both
central and local offices. This agency would report to the Ministry of Labor and Social
Protection, which would continu to have primary responsibility for policymaking on social
insurance and pension matters and for drafting social security legislation and monitoring its
implementation.
A new benefits stuce  for social insurance and pensions is recommended, in which
some of the current noncontributory benefits would be transferred to other agencies and new
benefits, needed both during and after the transition to a market economy, would be
established. It is recommended that pensions be indexed to protect pensioners' incomes
against the effects of inflation. And to increase the pension system's fairness and ensure its
solvency, it is recommended that the period of time for which eamings are used in
calculating pensions be gradually extended, that the retirement age also be gradually extended
and that it be made uniform, and that the income base to be used in determining social
insurance contributions, pensions, and other benefits be clearly defined. The chapter reports
the results of an analysis of the effects of these changes on contribution and pension levels,
taking into account demographic and economic projections.
The need to increase the system's transparency and fairness, to ease the burden on
employers and on the social insurance and pension staff-overwhelmed by the overly
complex benefit calculation-and to improve the distribution within and between generations
has been the impetus behind the proposed simplified pension calculation formula. This
formula should link contributions to benefits more closely and ensure continuity between the
present and future systems. Also proposed is a ceiling on the earnings considered in
establishing individual social insurance contribution rates and pension benefits.
In addition, the chapter analyzes the possibility of increasing not only old-age pensions, but
also disability pensions and short-term benefits, especially temporary disability benefits.
In evaluating social insurance contributions, the chapter suggests that responsibility
for contributions for employed persons be allocated between employers and employees, in
proportions that would differ among the work groups. Such an arrangement would leave



92
Annex 5
Page 8 of 8
employees' net salaries unaffected. The chapter,also analyzes the contributions of the self-
employed, including farmers.
The medium- and long-term financial effects of the proposed reforms are estimated
using complex mathematical models. The results suggest that the reforms would make it
possible either to reduce social insurance contributions if the present wage replacement rtes
are kept unchanged, or to icrease the wage replacement rates if the current contribution
rates are maintained.
Finally, the chapter lays out a strategy for developing a voluntary private pension
scheme to complement the public pension system. A private pension system would allow
people to add substantially to their reirement ncomes.
The last chapter puts forth a phased program of action for implementing the reforms
to the social insurance and pension system, detailing both technical and legislative steps. The
program outlines the conditions for the different steps and the relationships between them,
and gives an estimated schedule for their implementation.
x c x
It is our opinion that the discussions and suggestions provided in this white paper will
contribute toward the realization of the reforms of Romania's social insuLrance and pension
system.

ARTICLE 1
Q=Zp^;A STJLT NTW
The purpose of this protocol in to provide terms of reference for the
establishment and operation of a Career Information and Counseling Working
Group (CICWG) and a Career Information and Counseling Advisory Group (CICAG)
to implement the Career Information and Counseling subcomponent of the World
Bank Employment and Social Protection Project.
ARTICLE 2
STATEMNT OF PURPOS3 OF THE CICWG
The purpose of the CICWG is to develop high quality career information,
counseling materials and procedures to improve human resource allocation,
labor force mobility and productivity, and to improve the cost-effectiveness
of employment, education and training programs. The primary target groups for
the products of the CICWG will be unemployed adults in employment service
offices and students in schools. Since the Ministry of Labor and Social
Protection (MOLSP) and the Ministry of Education (MOE) are the major
stakeholders, they w$ll provide an equal number of experts to staff the CICKO.
STATZEMNT OF PURPOSE OF THS CICAG
The products of the CICWG will influence the career decisions of millions of
students and adults (unemployed and employed) each year and significantly
impact the Romanian economy. To ensure they accurately reflect current and
projected labor force realities, carefully selected experts from government,
business and industry, labor and user groups wi:l participate either as
regular or consulting members of the CICAG, and influence all stages of
conceptualization, development, validation, distribution and evaluation of
CICWG products.
ARTICLE 3
OR3AIZATION OF THE CICWG
The CICWG will be staffed by up to 10 full-time professionals, who will be
located in facilities provided by the Instituto for Educational Sciences
(IES). It will have a support staff of three people, provided by MOE/IRS.
The Director of the CICWG will be from MOLSP and the Deputy Director will be
from ZES. Reporting to them will be four experts appointed by MOLSP and four



94
Annex 6
Page 2 of 3
appointed by MOE. This staff will be supported occasionally by foreign
experts for short periods and more regularly by local experts either on
secondment from their own organizations or on contract.
ORgANZZAT!ON OF THE CXCAG
The CICAG wvil be comprised of at least 4 designated representatives from
MOLSP and an equal number from MOE, plus 1 representative of the Ministry of
Youth and Sports. Among these representatives will be a Secretary of State
from each ministry, at least one person at the Director General level from
each ministry, and other specialists deemed appropriate by each ministry. It
is anticipated that the CICAG will be enlarged over time as other interested
agencies seek participation, but their participation must. be improved
unanimously by the core group described above. This core group will be
consulted extensively with representatives of employer groups, union groups
and user groups to ensure that the products of the CICWG are congruent with
the needs of all social partners.
The CICAG will meet formally at least twice per year but its members will
always be as accessible as possible to CICWG staff on an advisory basis. They
will also help the CICWG organize career information and counseling review
committees within their organizations, and facilitate field-testing and
implementation of CICWG products. Any disputes concerning activities of the
CICWG will be settled by majority rule among CICAG members.
ARTICM. 4
PRODVCTS To EX DNvELOPD
The CICWG, supported by the CICAG, will develop:
-     Printed career information which will include occupational profiles,
career newspapers, posters and other products deemed important by CICHO,
CICAG and their partners.
-     Computer software which will be used in employment offices and schools
to help individuals explore their career options.
-     Video programs which will be used in employment offices and schools to
teach the processes of career planning and job search and introduce
viewers to occupational sectors of special importance to Romania's
emerging market economy.
-     Interest and aptitude tests, in print and computer-based formats, which
will be used in mployment offices and schools to help youth and adults
identify occupations compatible with their interests and aptitudes.
-     Short-term training courses to teach staff from employment offices and
schools how to use the above products effectively, and a four-month



95                             Annex 6
Page 3 of 3
university diploma program in career development and counseling to
enable counselors from employment offices and schools to counsel more
effectively.
ARTZCLZ S
PO08TIORB FOR C!cwo PERSONNEL
The staff of the CICWG will require special knowledge and skills not currently
available in Romania and they will be required to interact effectively with
large numbers of people .from throughout the country, work long hours and
endure considerable travel in the performance of their duties. The CICWG
positions will be sufficiently elevated to attract and keep the necessary
personnel on this team.
ARUTZrL 6
ZSTAZLZBsU  OF TNr CICWO/ICZaG
Membership of the CICAG will be established and the CICWG staff will be in
place in facilities provided by IES immediately. The CICWG will proceed with
a detailed review of the status of career counseling in other countries and
the development of a detailed CIC plan for Romania. As a concurrent activity,
project team members will present the CIC plan to local employment offices and
school administrators and staff to involve a broad cross-section of
professionals in the planning process.
ARTICLE 7
LEGISLATION
MOLSP and MOE will endeavor to include in any new legislation approved by
parliament, where appropriate, terminology which defines and supports the
outcomes of the career information and counseling program, particularly in
schools and labor offices.

Protocal for Establishment of the NTB
PROTOCOL
concerning the estabLishment and responsibilities of the National Training Board
1. Purpose of the Protocol
The purpose of this Protocol is to obtain agreement by the three social parters
(Government, Employers' organizations and Trade Unions) to set up a National Traning
Board, as a tipartite body to develop and implement the adult training policy and strategy
together.
2. Objectives of the National Training Board
* Promote cooperation among Gove;nment, Trade Unions and Employers (pubLic and
private) in adult training for employment and ensure development of a traini  system better
adapted to the demands of the market economy.
* Pmmote development of quality. lxible training progras at the level of groups of
counties, cities and towns in order to meet the existng and future needs for labor, with a
view to improving productivity and economic growth.
* Assure unrestricted access to trairing programs to companies willing to have their
work force trained, to individuals who are unemployed, unskilled youth leaving schools, or
any other individuals who are willing or obliged to get trained, retrained or improve their
skills, while employed with commercial companies, regional autonomies, public institutions,
the co-operative or the private sector.
3. Role of National Training Board
* The National Training Board is the mational tripartite body for discussing and
proposing solutions for the adult training poIi.cy and priorities of the national and regional
economy in the field.
* Within the regulations in force, the rational Training Board will emblish short and
medium-term priorities and endorse funding by the Govermment in order to implement the
training programs according to the needs of every phase, such as: taining for reconversion
of certain industries, mass lay-offs in certain industries and geographical regions,
management, entrepreneurship training and programs of national signifcance.
* The National Training Board may propose the Government amendments to the
existing training legislation.



98
Page 2 of 3
* The National Training Board will coordinate activity of tripartite Couty Training
Boards (CTBs) to develop the policy and prionties of taining at the local level, including the
needs for funding in this field. The CTBs will submit to the National Traiing BQard
justfied proposals to establish some adult training centers managed by independent
foundations.
* Setting up and developing taining centers fianced by the state will be possible on
condition that the criteria sipulated in point 5 are met.
4. Role of the MOLSP in its relationship with the Natdonal Training Board
In this relationship with the National Traning Board, the MOISP will:
* ensure that implementation of decisions made by the National Training Board fall
within the legal and administaive framework set by the Govenmment and Parliament;
* be rsponsible for reng, obtaining and adminiser  the budgetary allocations
and any other financig sources (bilateral or multilateral) to accomplish activities endorsd by
the National Training Boards;
* be responsible for providing the staff and administrative services needed for the
activities of the National Training Board, as well as for implementing the actions proposed
and endorsed by this Board.
= The munber of saff for the National Training Board's activities will be 17, headed
by a Director appointed by the MOISP in agreement with the National Train   Board and
rpoting both to the chair of the National Training Board and to the MOISP
* The MOISP will nominte an interim chair ntil - withi one year - the N ational
Trainng Board nomiates one. The chair will be elected by rotation among the social
parters, for a period of one year.
S. CrIeria for fincing of trining actiies
The National Training Board will endorse financing of establishent and
development of regional or local training centers as well as financing of local trining
programs of condition that the following minimum criteria are met
-     training must be modular, individualized, in a flexible entry/exit manner;
-     training must be bgsed as a rule on regional and local needs for labor;
-     regional and local centers must be able to provide a minimum range of
services, as follows: occupational counselling and placement when trainig is
completed; a range of minimum 10 occupational taining profiles; general
education courses to support vocational traidng; custom training of
enterprises, small business training. Centers may subcontract with specialized
private cooperative or public compaies for delivery of certai services
mentioned above, which they canot provide tbemselves.
* The National Tramning Board may establish additional criteria.



99
Annex 7
Page 3 of 3
6. Hnancing of Training
* The financing sources for training may be: the unemployment fund, the state
budget, intemational organizations, companies and individual clients.
* Financing of training centers will be accomplished on the basis of normative tariffs
per trainee, set in accordance with the methodological norms to be endorsed by the National
Training Board.
* Periodically and at least once every year, the number of trainees in the training
courses as well as the extent to which the prerequisite criteria have been met, will be
checked. If the number of trainees or prerequisite criteria are not met, the MOLSP may
decide upon withdrawal of a portion of funds allocated.
7. National Training Board Membership
* The National Training Board will be composed of representatives of three social
partners: (a) ministries and other government institutions; (b) representatives of employers in
the private and public sectors and (c) representatives of national Trade Union Confederations.
The numbers of trade union and employers' organizations that may sign the Protocol is
unrestricted, while each social partner will have a maximum number of 7 votes. The 7
voting representatives of each of the social partners will be agreed upon by each group by 31
July 1993.
* Each of partners will have an equal share of votes; decisions will require a two-
thirds majority vote, and a quorum of two thirds of the members.
* The National Training Board will meet at least once every quarter and also
whenever necessary.
This Protocol is signed on 9 July 1993 by the following individuals:

partially offset the costs of retraining employed workers in
recognition that this Is an investment in human capital with
positive social externalities.
4.    Fees or user charges paid by employed individuals and firms, would
be used to offset all or a major part (i.e. 50 percent) of the
costs of training employed workers and managers at all
institutions (e.g., RATCs, adult training).

DRAFT CRITERIA FOR SELECTION OF MANAGEMENT TRAINING PROGRAMS1
A.    Itroduction
Zconomic reStructuring will require skillful and innovative management on
the part of firms, but current inadequate managerial skills pone a serious
constraint to firms successfully responding to the new comp titive environment.
Romanian managers learned their profession in a centralized system where their
objectives related to meeting production targets. Many managers are engineers,
well trained technically, but lacking in knowledge of modern financial
management, human resources management and marketing techniques. Nevertheless,
they are expected to exercise initiative in strategic planning and decision-
making requiring skills in a competitive and dynamic environment. While the
universities and polytechnics are beginning to adapt business, management and
economics curricula, this will take time and will meet mainly the needs of young
people currently enrolled.   While,  larger enterprises can often get their
management consulting and training needs met through joint ventures with foreign
partners, mall- and medium-size enterprises lack access to practically-oriented
management training.
In order to develop the specific management skills and competencies
required by enterprises for achieving a competitive position in the new market
economy, the NTB would define policies, conditions and procedures for the
development of management training for private enterprises, especially small- and
medium-size ones. The NTB would: (a) establish priorities and guidelines to
promote development of management training, develop short- and medium-term plans,
and train a small staff to implement/monitor assistance programs; (b) assist
selected enterprises in effectively formulating their management training needs;
and (c) select and support institutions to develop and supply management training
programs corresponding to those needs.  The project would provide short-term
(about six months) training for managers of about 200 firms.  The NTB would
periodically review program impact and adapt priorities as necessary (see
sections B and C below).
B.     Manaaement Training Needs: Criteria for Enterorises
The following criteria will be used by the NTB in selecting and endorsing
enterprises to receive management training.
1.    Minimum Eligibility Criteria for Enterprises
(Note: Enterprises must mest these criteria to be eligible for selection)
Management Training was included in the original design of the Adult
Training subcomponent, but cofinancing for Management Training was not available
at the time of Negotiations. Thus, the MOLSP, with Bank assistance, is still
seeking bilateral grants to support the Management Training Programs deacribed
in this annex.



106
Annex 8-a
Page 2 of 6
Status and size:   Only private enterprises  (majority privato
ownership and control) incorporated and registered under Romanian
business law are eligible. The maximum size is 100 employees, i.e.,
small to medium scale. No less than 60 percent of the funds are to
be allocated to firms with 100 or less employees.
ownership: Enterprises must have a two-thirds ownership by private
Romanian  capital.       Participation  of  foreign  subsidiario-
incorporated in Romania are to be considered foreign.   Foreign
franchises even if owned by Romanian private interests are not
eligible.
Taxes:   Enterprises must have paid their participation to the
employment tax fund.
2.    Criteria on which Proposals will be Evaluated
Relevance of the Propogal: The enterprise's proposal for
identification of management training needs must be consistent with
itg plans or strategies of development.
Financial Ca2abilitv:   The enterprise must have the financial
capability (directly and/or with bank loans) to carry out its plans
including to finance its share of technical assistance, management
restructuring and training.
Manaaement Structure:   The existing management structure must
demonstrate it's capacity to undergo change.
Sector of Activity: Certain strategic sectors may be given priority
(e.g., export oriented activities, employment creation).
Geoaravhic Area: Proposals from under represented geographic area
may be given priority.
C.     Manacement Trainina Provision: Criteria for Trainina Institutions
1.   Minimum Eligibility Criteria for Training Institutions
(Note: Training Institutions must meet theme criteria to be eligible for
selection).
Status:  Public and private institutions must have training or
education as their primary activity to be eligible. Public training
institutions which were developed as part of large public companies
must be autonomous.  Private companies must be incorporated and
registered under Romanian business law.



107
&Dnex B-B
Page 3 of 6
ownershi2: All institutions must have full Romanian ownership.
Moreover, they cannot be owned or controlled by either employers or
workers' unions.
Taxes: All institutions must have fully paid their participation to
the employment tax fund.
2.    Criteria on which Proposals will be Evaluated
Relevance of the ProDosal: Proposals must correspond to specific
demand identified in enterprises, preferably by technical assistance
provided in the previous phase of the program.
Methodoloav:   Proposals must be based on the development of
approaches, methodologies and tools which are applicable to
Romanian enterprises in different areas of activity.
Calpabilitv to Realize Proiect: Institutions must demonstrate they
have the structural, human, and financial capability to develop and
Implement their proposals.
Cumulative Suwoort: Institutions must give all information on other
assistance they may receive for the same proposal. Priority will be
given to proposals which do not have other support.
Cost-effectiveness: Proposals must demonstrate cost-effectiveness
in their implementation (for the institution itself asd its
customers).
Field of Training:  Priority may be given to fields of training
which are in high demand in enterprises in reference to the results
of technical assistance in the previous phase of the program.
D.    Princiolem of Financina Above Trainina Proarams IB and Cl.
1.    All   project   investment   resources   allocated   for   local
institutions/sites, individual adult and management training
programs would be controlled by the NTB and disbursed according to
criteria and procedures agreed with the Bank.
2.    Recurrent coats for the small contingent of NTB staff would be
covered from the State budget. Recurrent costs (i.e., unit costs of
training) would be financed from multiple sources which would vary
depending on the institution providing the service and client group
served.
3.    State  budget  resources  would  finance  training  the  long-term
unemployed or school leavers for which there is a public good

justification. State budget resources would also be used to
partially offset the costs of retraining employed workers in
recognition that this is an investment in human capital with
posltive social externalities.
4.    Fees or user charges paid by employed individuals and firms, would
be used to offset all or part (at least 50 percent) of the costs of
training employed workers and managers at all institutions (e.g.,
RATCu, adult training, management training).

including establishing: (a) a
tripartite governance at the regional
and/or Judet level to govern RATCs;
(b) a financial structure to support
investment and recurrent costs of
training programs for different
clients (e.g. adults seeking initial
employment, seeking to change their
qualifications, and the unemployed);
(c) minimum services to be provided in
RATCs (job counseling and placement,
general education, occupational
training, entrepreneurship training,
custom enterprise training); (d)
methods of delivering services
training services (open entry to and
flexible entry exit modular
instruction in individual programs in
RATCs); (e) methods of financing
priority national programs and RATC
services (e.g. normative funding base
on the number of full-time equivalent
trainees).
(iii) Plan; Create a short-term (1
year) and longAC3!MITSE9ear) plan to
Support adult training encompassing:
